prcpg10{_}1259{_}That's all at an end now!
prcpg10{_}1260{_}Well, back to our camp to make the best of the world they have left behind them!"
prcpg10{_}1261{_}And then in a tone that Richard was not meant to hear, "While mi dona Leonor remains to me there is something saintly and softening still in this world!
prcpg10{_}1262{_}Heaven help me--ay, and all my foes--were she gone from it too!"
prcpg10{_}1263{_}CHAPTER VIII--RICHARD'S WRAITH    "No distance breaks the tie of blood; Brothers are brothers evermore; Nor wrong, nor wrath of deadliest mood, That magic may o'erpower."
prcpg10{_}1264{_}--Christian Year.
prcpg10{_}1265{_}It was nearly dark when the Prince and the Page landed on the island, and found the tents already set up in their due order and rank, according to the discipline that no one durst transgress where Edward was the commander.
prcpg10{_}1266{_}Richard attended him to his pavilion, and being there dismissed until supper-time, crossed the square space which was always left around the royal banner, to the tent at the southern corner, which was regularly appropriated to the pages' use.
prcpg10{_}1267{_}On lifting its curtain he was, however, dismayed to see a kirtle there, and imagining that he must have fallen upon the ladies' quarters, he was retreating with an apology; when the sharp voice of Dame Idonea called out, "Oh yes, Master Page!
prcpg10{_}1268{_}'tis you that are at home here.
prcpg10{_}1269{_}I was merely tarrying till 'twas the will of one of you to come in and look to the poor child."
prcpg10{_}1270{_}And little John of Dunster called from a couch of mantles, "Richard, oh!
prcpg10{_}1271{_}is it he at last?"
prcpg10{_}1272{_}"It is I," said Richard, advancing into the light of a brass lamp, hung by chains from the top of the tent.
prcpg10{_}1273{_}"This is kind indeed, Lady!
prcpg10{_}1274{_}But is he indeed so ill at ease?"
prcpg10{_}1275{_}"How should he be otherwise, with none of you idle-pated pages casting a thought to him?"
prcpg10{_}1276{_}"I was grieved to leave him--but the Prince summoned me," began Richard.
prcpg10{_}1277{_}"Beshrew thee!
prcpg10{_}1278{_}Tell me not of princes, as though there were no one whom thou couldst bid to have a care of the little lad!"
prcpg10{_}1279{_}"I did bid Piers--," Richard made another attempt.
prcpg10{_}1280{_}"Piers, quotha?
prcpg10{_}1281{_}Why didst not bid the Jackanapes that sits on the luggage?
prcpg10{_}1282{_}A proper warder for a sick babe!"
prcpg10{_}1283{_}"I am no babe!"
prcpg10{_}1284{_}here burst out John; "I am twelve years old come Martinmas, and I need no tendance but Richard's."
prcpg10{_}1285{_}"Ha, ha!
prcpg10{_}1286{_}So those are all the thanks we ladies get, when we are not young and fair!"
prcpg10{_}1287{_}laughed Dame Idonea, rather amused.
prcpg10{_}1288{_}"I want no women, young or old," petulantly repeated John; "I want Richard.--Lift me up, Richard; take away this cloak."
prcpg10{_}1289{_}"For his life, no!"
prcpg10{_}1290{_}returned the Dame; "he has the heats and the chills on him, and to let him take cold would be mere slaughter."
prcpg10{_}1291{_}"Alas!"
prcpg10{_}1292{_}said Richard, "I hoped nothing ailed him but the sea, and that landing would make all well."
prcpg10{_}1293{_}"As if the sea ever made a child shiver and burn by turns!
prcpg10{_}1294{_}Nay, 'tis the trick of the sun in these parts.
prcpg10{_}1295{_}Strange that the sun himself should be a mere ally of the Infidel!
prcpg10{_}1296{_}I tell thee, if the child is ever to see Dunster again, thou must watch him well, keep him from the sun by day and the chill by night; or he'll be like the poor creatures in the French camp out there, whom, I suppose, you found in fine case."
prcpg10{_}1297{_}"Alack yes, Lady!"
prcpg10{_}1298{_}"I've seen it many a time; and all their disorders will be creeping into our camp next.
prcpg10{_}1299{_}Tell me, is it even as they told us, one king dead and the other dying?"
prcpg10{_}1300{_}Richard began to wonder whether he should ever get her out of his tent, for she insisted on his telling her every possible particular-- who had died, who had lived, who was sick, who well; and as from the close connection between the English, French, and Sicilian courts, whose queens were all sisters, she knew who every one was, and accounted for the history of each person she inquired after, back to the last generation--happy if it were not to the third--her conversation was not quickly over.
prcpg10{_}1301{_}She ended at last, by desiring Richard to give her patient some of a febrifuge, which she had brought with her, every two hours, and when it was all spent, or in case of any change in the boy's state, to summon her from the ladies' tent; adding, however, "But what's the use of leaving a pert springald like thee in charge?
prcpg10{_}1302{_}Thou wilt sleep like a very dormouse, I'll warrant!
prcpg10{_}1303{_}I'd best call Mother Jugge."
prcpg10{_}1304{_}"Oh no, no!"
prcpg10{_}1305{_}cried John; to whom the attendance of Mother Jugge would have been a worse indignity than the being nursed by Dame Idonea; "let me have no one but Richard!
prcpg10{_}1306{_}Richard knows all I want.--Richard, leave me not again."
prcpg10{_}1307{_}"Ay, ay; a little lad ever hangs to a bigger, were he to torture the life out of him.
prcpg10{_}1308{_}Small thanks for us women after our good looks be past.
prcpg10{_}1309{_}But I'll look in on the child in early morn, thanks or no thanks; for I know his mother well, and if I can help it, the hyenas shall not make game of his bones, as I hear them doing by the French yonder."
prcpg10{_}1310{_}John strove to say that, indeed, he thanked her, and had been infinitely comforted and refreshed by her care, and that all he meant was to express his distaste to Mother Jugge, the lavender (i.e.
prcpg10{_}1311{_}laundress), and his desire for Richard Fowen's company; but he was little attended to, and apparently more than half offended, the brisk old lady trotted away.
prcpg10{_}1312{_}That island was a dreary place; without a tree or any shelter from the glare of sun and sea, whose combined influences threatened blindness, sun-stroke, or at the very least blistered the faces of those who stepped beyond their tents by day.
prcpg10{_}1313{_}The Prince's orders, however, strictly confined his army within its bounds, except that at twilight parties were sent ashore for water and provisions, under strict orders, however, to hold no parley with any one from the French or Sicilian camps, lest they should bring home the infection of the pestilence; and always under the command of some trustworthy knight, able and willing to enforce the command.
prcpg10{_}1314{_}The Prince himself refused all participation in the counsels of Charles of Anjou, and confined himself, like his men, entirely to the fleet and island.
prcpg10{_}1315{_}Charles contrived to spread a report, that his displeasure was solely due to his disappointment at being balked of fighting with the Tunisians; and that instead of indignant grief at the perversion of the wrecked Crusade, he was only showing the sullenness of an aggrieved swordsman.
prcpg10{_}1316{_}Even young Philippe le Hardi, a dull, heavy, ignorant youth, was led to suppose this was the cause of his offence, and though daily inquiries were sent through the Genoese crews for his health, he made no demonstration of willingness to see his cousin of England.
prcpg10{_}1317{_}Thus Richard had no opportunity of ascertaining whether there were any basis for the strange impression he had received in St. Louis's death-chamber.
prcpg10{_}1318{_}It would have been an act of disobedience, not soon overlooked by the Prince, had one of his immediate suite transgressed his commands, and indeed, so strict was the discipline, that it would scarcely have been possible to make the attempt.
prcpg10{_}1319{_}Besides, Richard's time was entirely engrossed between his duties in attending on the Prince, and his care of little John of Dunster, who had a sharp attack of fever, and was no doubt only carried through it by the experienced skill of Dame Idonea Osbright, and by Richard's tender nursing.
prcpg10{_}1320{_}Somehow the dame's heart was not won, even by the elder page's dutiful care and obedience to all her directions.
prcpg10{_}1321{_}Partly she viewed him as a rival in the affections of the patient--who, poor little fellow, would in his companion's absence be the child he was, and let her treat him like his mother, or old nurse, chattering to her freely about home, and his home-sick longings; whereas the instant any male companion appeared, he made it a point of honour to be the manly warrior and crusader, just succeeding so far as to be sullen instead of plaintive; though when left to Richard, he could again relax his dignity, and become natural and affectionate.
prcpg10{_}1322{_}But besides this species of jealousy, Richard suspected that Lady Osbright knew, or at least guessed, his own parentage, and disliked him for it accordingly.
prcpg10{_}1323{_}She had never forgotten the distress and degradation of his mother's stolen marriage, nor forgiven his father for it; she had often stung the proud heart of his brother Henry, when he shared the nursery of his cousins the princes; and her sturdy English dislike of foreigners, and her strong narrow personal loyalty, had alike resulted in the most vehement hatred of the Earl of Leicester, whose head she would assuredly have welcomed with barbarous exultation, worthy of her Danish ancestors.
prcpg10{_}1324{_}Little chance, then, was there that she would regard with favour his son under a feigned name, fostered in the Prince's own court and camp.
prcpg10{_}1325{_}She was a constraint, and almost a vexation, to Richard, and he heartily wished that the boy's recovery would free his tent from her.
prcpg10{_}1326{_}The boy did recover favourably, in spite of all the discomforts of the island, and was decidedly convalescent when, after nearly ten days' isolation on the island, Edward drew out his whole force upon the shore to do honour to the embarkation of the relics of Louis IX.
prcpg10{_}1327{_}It was one of the most solemn and melancholy pageants that could be conceived.
prcpg10{_}1328{_}A wide lane of mailed soldiers was drawn up, Sicilians and Provencals on the one side, and on the other, English and the Knights of the two Orders.
prcpg10{_}1329{_}All stood, or sat on horseback in shining steel, guarding the way along which were carried the coffins.
prcpg10{_}1330{_}In memory, perhaps, of Louis's own words, "I, your leader, am going first," his remains headed the procession, closely followed by those of his young son; and behind it marched his two brothers, Charles and Alfonse, and his son-in-law, the King of Navarre (the two latter already bearing the seeds of the fatal malady), and the three English princes, Edward, Edmund, and Henry of Almayne, each followed by his immediate suite.
prcpg10{_}1331{_}The long line of coffins of French counts and nobles, whose lives had in like manner been sacrificed, brought up the rear; and alas!
prcpg10{_}1332{_}how many nameless dead must have been left in the ruins!
prcpg10{_}1333{_}Each coffin when brought to the shore was placed in a boat, and with muffled oars transplanted to the vessel ready to receive it, while the troops remained drawn up on the shore.
prcpg10{_}1334{_}The procession that ensued was almost more mournful.
prcpg10{_}1335{_}It was still of biers, but these were not of the dead but of the living, and again the foremost was the King of France, while next to him came his sister, the Queen of Navarre.
prcpg10{_}1336{_}Edward went down to his litter, as it was brought on the beach, and offered him his arm as he feebly stepped forth to enter the boat.
prcpg10{_}1337{_}Philippe looked up to his tall cousin, and wrung his hands as he murmured, "Alas!
prcpg10{_}1338{_}what is to be the end of all this?"
prcpg10{_}1339{_}Edward made kind and cheerful reply, that things would look better when they met at Trapani, and then almost lifted the young king into his boat.
prcpg10{_}1340{_}Poor youth, he had not yet seen the end!
prcpg10{_}1341{_}He was yet to lose his wife, his brother-in-law, and his uncle and aunt, ere he should see his home again.
prcpg10{_}1342{_}Richard and Hamlyn de Valence, as part of the Prince's train, had moved in the procession; and they were for the rest of the day in close attendance on their lord, conveying his numerous orders for the embarkation of the troops on the morrow, on their return to Sicily.
prcpg10{_}1343{_}It was not till night-fall that Richard returned to his tent, where John of Dunster was sitting on the sand at the door, eagerly watching for him.
prcpg10{_}1344{_}"Well, Jack, my lad, how hast thou sped?"
prcpg10{_}1345{_}asked he, advancing.
prcpg10{_}1346{_}"Couldst see our doleful array?"
prcpg10{_}1347{_}"Is it thou, indeed, this time?"
prcpg10{_}1348{_}said the boy, catching at his cloak.
prcpg10{_}1349{_}"Why, who should it be?"
prcpg10{_}1350{_}"Thy wraith!
prcpg10{_}1351{_}Thy double-ganger has been here Richard."
prcpg10{_}1352{_}"What, dreaming again?"
prcpg10{_}1353{_}'No no!
prcpg10{_}1354{_}I am well, I am strong.
prcpg10{_}1355{_}But this IS the land of enchantment!
prcpg10{_}1356{_}Thou knowst it is.
prcpg10{_}1357{_}Did we not see a fleet of fairy boats sailing on the sea?
prcpg10{_}1358{_}and a leaf eat up a fly here on this very tent pole?
prcpg10{_}1359{_}And did not the Fay Morgaine show us towns and castles and churches in the sea?
prcpg10{_}1360{_}Thou didst not call me light-headed then, Richard; thou sawest it too!"
prcpg10{_}1361{_}"But this wraith of mine!
prcpg10{_}1362{_}Where didst see it?"
prcpg10{_}1363{_}"In this tent.
prcpg10{_}1364{_}I was lying on the sand, trying if I could make it hold enough to build a castle of it, when the curtain was put back, and there thou stoodest, Richard!"
prcpg10{_}1365{_}"Well, did I speak or vanish?"
prcpg10{_}1366{_}"Oh, thou spakest--I mean the THING spake, and it said, 'Is this the tent of the young Lord of Montfort?'
prcpg10{_}1367{_}How now--what have I said?"
prcpg10{_}1368{_}"Whom did he ask for?"
prcpg10{_}1369{_}demanded Richard breathlessly.
prcpg10{_}1370{_}"Montfort--young Lord de Montfort!"
prcpg10{_}1371{_}replied John; "I know it was, for he said it twice over."
prcpg10{_}1372{_}"And what didst thou answer?"
prcpg10{_}1373{_}"What should I answer?
prcpg10{_}1374{_}I said we had no Montforts here; for they were all dishonoured traitors, slain and outlawed."
prcpg10{_}1375{_}Richard could not restrain a sudden indignant exclamation that startled the boy.
prcpg10{_}1376{_}"Every one says so!
prcpg10{_}1377{_}My father says so!"
prcpg10{_}1378{_}he returned, somewhat defiantly.
prcpg10{_}1379{_}"Not of the Earl," said Richard, recollecting himself.
prcpg10{_}1380{_}"He said every one of the young Montforts was a foul traitor, and man-sworn tyrant, as bad as King John had been ere the Charter," repeated John hotly, "and their father was as bad, since he would give no redress.
prcpg10{_}1381{_}Thou knowst how they served us in Somerset and Devon!"
prcpg10{_}1382{_}"I have heard, I have heard," said Richard, cutting short the story, and controlling his own burning pain, glad that the darkness concealed his face.
prcpg10{_}1383{_}"No more of that; but tell me, what said this stranger?"
prcpg10{_}1384{_}"Thou thinkest it was really a stranger, and not thy wraith?"
prcpg10{_}1385{_}said John anxiously.
prcpg10{_}1386{_}"I hope it was, for Dame Idonea said if it were a wraith, it betokened that thou wouldst not--live long--and oh, Richard!
prcpg10{_}1387{_}I could not spare thee!"
prcpg10{_}1388{_}And the little fellow came nestling up to his friend's breast in an access of tenderness, such as perhaps he would have disdained save in the darkness.
prcpg10{_}1389{_}"Did Dame Idonea see him?"
prcpg10{_}1390{_}asked Richard.
prcpg10{_}1391{_}"No; but she came in soon after he had vanished."
prcpg10{_}1392{_}"Vanished!
prcpg10{_}1393{_}What, like Fay Morgaine's castles?
prcpg10{_}1394{_}Tell me in sooth, John; it imports me to know.
prcpg10{_}1395{_}What did this stranger, when thou spakest thus of the House of Montfort?"
prcpg10{_}1396{_}"He answered," said John; "he did not answer courteously--he said, that I was a malapert little ass, and demanded again where this young Montfort's tent was.
prcpg10{_}1397{_}So then I said, that if a Montfort dared to show his traitor's face in this camp, the Prince would hang him as high as Judas; for I wanted to be rid of him, Richard!
prcpg10{_}1398{_}it was so dreadful to see thy face, and hear thy voice talking French, and asking for dead traitors."
prcpg10{_}1399{_}"French!"
prcpg10{_}1400{_}said Richard.
prcpg10{_}1401{_}"Methought thou knewst no French!"
prcpg10{_}1402{_}"I--I have heard it long now, more's the pity," faltered John, "and-- and I'd have spoken anything to be rid of that shape."
prcpg10{_}1403{_}"And wert thou rid?
prcpg10{_}1404{_}What befell then?"
prcpg10{_}1405{_}"It cursed the Prince, and King, and all of them," said John with a shudder; "it looked black and deadly, and I crossed myself, and said the Blessed Name, and no doubt it writhed itself and went off in brimstone and smoke, for I shut my eyes, and when I looked up again it was gone!"
prcpg10{_}1406{_}"Gone!
prcpg10{_}1407{_}Didst look after him?"
prcpg10{_}1408{_}"Oh, no!
prcpg10{_}1409{_}Earthly things are all food for a brave man's sword," said Master John, drawing himself up very valiantly, "but wraiths and things from beneath--they do scare the very heart out of a man.
prcpg10{_}1410{_}And I lay, I don't know how, till Dame Idonea came in; and she said either the foul fiend had put on thy shape because he boded thee ill, or it was one of the traitor brood looking for his like."
prcpg10{_}1411{_}"Tell me, John," said Richard anxiously; "surely he was not in all points like me.
prcpg10{_}1412{_}Had he our English white cross?"
prcpg10{_}1413{_}"I cannot say as to the cross," said John; "meseemed it was all you-- yourself--and that was all--only I thought your voice was strange and hollow--and--now I think of it--yes--he was bearded--brown bearded.
prcpg10{_}1414{_}And," with a sudden thought, "stand up, prithee, in the opening of the tent;" and then taking his post where he had been sitting at the time of the apparition, "He was not so tall as thou art.
prcpg10{_}1415{_}Thy head comes above the fold of the curtain, and his, I know, did not touch it, for I saw the light over it.
prcpg10{_}1416{_}Then thou dost not think it was thy wraith?"
prcpg10{_}1417{_}he added anxiously.
prcpg10{_}1418{_}"I think my wraith would have measured me more exactly both in stature and in age," said Richard lightly.
prcpg10{_}1419{_}"But how did Leonillo comport himself?
prcpg10{_}1420{_}He brooks not a stranger in general; and dogs cannot endure the presence of a spirit."
prcpg10{_}1421{_}"Ah!
prcpg10{_}1422{_}but he fawned upon this one, and thrust his nose into his hand," said John, "and I think he must have run after him; for it was so long ere he came back to me, that I had feared greatly he was gone, and oh, Richard!
prcpg10{_}1423{_}then I must have gone too!
prcpg10{_}1424{_}I could never have met you without Leonillo."
prcpg10{_}1425{_}By this time Richard had little doubt that the visitor must have been one of his brothers, Simon or Guy, who were not unlikely to be among the Provencals, in the army of Charles of Anjou.
prcpg10{_}1426{_}He had not been thought to resemble them as a boy, but he had observed how much more alike brothers appear to strangers than they do to their own family; and he knew by occasional observations from the Prince, as well as from his brother Henry's recognition of his voice, that the old Montfort characteristics must be strong in himself.
prcpg10{_}1427{_}He would not, however, avow his belief to John of Dunster.
prcpg10{_}1428{_}Secrecy on his own birth had been enjoined on him by his uncle the King; and disobedience to the old man's most trifling commands was always sharply resented by the Prince; nor was the boy's view of the House of Montfort very favourable to such a declaration.
prcpg10{_}1429{_}Richard really loved the brave little fellow, and trusted that some day when the discovery must be made, it would be coupled with some exploit that would show it was no name to be ashamed of.
prcpg10{_}1430{_}So he only told the boy that he had no doubt the stranger was a foreign knight, who had once known the old Leicester family; but bade him mention the circumstance to no one.
prcpg10{_}1431{_}He feared, however, that the caution came too late, since Dame Idonea was not only an inveterate gossip, but was likely to hold in direful suspicion any one who had been inquired for by such a name.
prcpg10{_}1432{_}The personal disappointment of having missed his brother was great.
prcpg10{_}1433{_}Richard was very lonely.
prcpg10{_}1434{_}The Princes, and Hamlyn de Valence, were the only persons who knew his secret, and both by Prince Edmund and De Valence he was treated with indifference or dislike.
prcpg10{_}1435{_}Edward himself, though the object of his fervent affection, and his protector in all essentials, was of a reserved nature, and kept all his attendants at a great distance.
prcpg10{_}1436{_}On very rare occasions, when his feelings had been strongly stirred--as in the instance of his visit to his uncle's death-chamber--he might sometimes unbend; and momentary flashes from the glow of his warm deep heart went further in securing the love and devotion of those around him, than would the daily affability of a lower nature; but in ordinary life, towards all concerned with him except his nearest relations, he was a strict, cold, grave disciplinarian, ever just, though on the side of severity, and stern towards the slightest neglect or breach of observance, nor did he make any exception in favour of Richard.
prcpg10{_}1437{_}If the youth seldom received one of his brief annihilating reproofs, it was because they were scarcely ever merited; but he had experienced that any want of exactitude in his duties was quite as severely visited as if he had not been the Prince's close kinsman, romantically rescued by him, and placed near his person by his special desire.
prcpg10{_}1438{_}And Eleanor, with all her gentle courtesy and kindness, was strictly withheld by her husband from pampering or cockering his pages; nor did she ever transgress his will.
prcpg10{_}1439{_}The atmosphere was perhaps bracing, but it was bleak:  and there were times when Richard regretted his acceptance of the Prince's offer, and yearned after family ties, equality, and freedom.
prcpg10{_}1440{_}Simon and Guy had never been kind to him, but at least they were his brothers, and with them disguise and constraint would be over--he should, too, be in communication with his mother and sister.
prcpg10{_}1441{_}He was strongly inclined to cast in his lot with them, and end this life of secrecy, and distrust from all around him save one, and his loyal love ill requited even by that one.
prcpg10{_}1442{_}It grieved him keenly that one of his brothers should have been repulsed from his tent; an absolutely famished longing for fraternal intercourse gained possession of him, and as he lay on his pallet that night in the dark, he even shed tears at the thought of the greeting and embrace that he had missed.
prcpg10{_}1443{_}Still he had hopes for the future.
prcpg10{_}1444{_}There must be meetings and possibilities of inquiries passing between the three armies, and he would let no opportunity go by.
prcpg10{_}1445{_}The next day, however, there was no chance.
prcpg10{_}1446{_}The English troops were embarked in their vessels, and after a short and prosperous passage were again landed at Trapani, the western angle of Sicily.
prcpg10{_}1447{_}The French had sailed first, but were not in harbour when the English came in; and the Sicilians, who had brought up the rear, arrived the next day, but still there was no tidings of the French.
prcpg10{_}1448{_}Towards the evening, however, the royal vessel bearing Philippe III.
prcpg10{_}1449{_}came into harbour, and all the rest were in sight, when at sunset a frightful storm arose, and the ships were in fearful case.
prcpg10{_}1450{_}Many foundered, many were wrecked on the rocky islets around the port, and the French army was almost as much reduced in numbers as it had been by the Plague of Carthage.
prcpg10{_}1451{_}Charles of Anjou remained himself in the town of Trapani, but knowing the evils of crowding a small space with troops, he at once sent his men inland, and Richard was again disappointed of the hope of seeing or hearing of his brothers; for the Prince still forbade all intercourse with the shattered remnant of the French army, justly dreading that they might still carry about them the seeds of the infection of the camp.
prcpg10{_}1452{_}The three heads of the Crusade, however, met in the Castle of Trapani to hold council on their future proceedings.
prcpg10{_}1453{_}The place was the state-chamber of the castle.
prcpg10{_}1454{_}Each prince had brought with him a single attendant, and the three stood in waiting near the door, in full view of their lords, though out of earshot.
prcpg10{_}1455{_}It was an opportunity that Richard could not bear to miss of asking for his brothers, unheard by any of those English ears who would be suspicious about his solicitude for the House of Montfort.
prcpg10{_}1456{_}A lively-looking Neapolitan lad was the attendant of King Charles; and in spite of all the perils of attempting conversation while thus waiting, Richard had--while the princes were greeting one another, and taking their seats--ventured the question, whether any of the sons of the English Earl of Leicester were in the Sicilian army.
prcpg10{_}1457{_}Of Earl of Leicester the Italian knew nothing; but Count of Montfort was a more familiar sound.
prcpg10{_}1458{_}"Si, si, vero!"
prcpg10{_}1459{_}Sicily had rung with it; and Count Rosso Aldobrandini, of the Maremma Toscana, had given his only daughter and heiress to the banished English knight, Guido di Monforte, who had served in the king's army as a Provencal.
prcpg10{_}1460{_}Richard's heart beat high.
prcpg10{_}1461{_}Guy a well-endowed count, with a castle, lands, and home!
prcpg10{_}1462{_}He would have asked where Guy now was, and how far off was the Maremma; but the conference between the princes was actually commencing, and silence became necessary on the part of their attendants.
prcpg10{_}1463{_}They could only hear the murmur of voices; but could discern plainly the keen looks and animated gestures of Charles of Anjou, the sickly sullen indifference of Philippe, and the majestic gravity of Edward, whose noble head towered above the other two as if he were their natural judge.
prcpg10{_}1464{_}Charles was, in fact, trying to persuade the others to sail with him for Greece, and there turn their forces on the unfortunate Michael Palaeologos, who had lately recovered Constantinople, the Empire that Charles hoped to win for himself, the favoured champion of Rome.
prcpg10{_}1465{_}Philippe merely replied that he had had enough of crusading, he was sick and weary, he must go home and bury his father, and get himself crowned.
prcpg10{_}1466{_}Charles might be then seen trying a little hypocrisy; and telling Philippe that his saintly father would only have wished to speed him on the way of the Cross.
prcpg10{_}1467{_}Then that trumpet voice of Edward, whose tones Richard never missed, answered, "What is the way of the Cross, fair uncle?"
prcpg10{_}1468{_}It was well known that Louis IX.
prcpg10{_}1469{_}had refused to crusade against Christians, even Greek Christians, and Philippe soon sheltered himself under the plea that had not at first occurred to his dull mind.
prcpg10{_}1470{_}In effect, he laid particulars before his uncle, that quickly made it plain that the French army was in too miserable a condition to do anything but return home; and Charles then addressed his persuasions to Edward--striving to convince him in the first place of the sanctity of a war against Greek heretics, and when Edward proved past being persuaded that arms meant for the recovery of the Holy Sepulchre ought not to be employed against Christians who reverenced it, he tried to demonstrate the uselessness of hoping to conquer the Holy Land, even by such a Crusade as had been at first planned, far less with the few attached to Edward's individual banner.
prcpg10{_}1471{_}Long did the king argue on.
prcpg10{_}1472{_}His low voice was scarcely audible, even without the words; but Edward's brief, ringing, almost scornful, replies, never failed to reach Richard's ear, and the last of them was, "It skills not, my fair uncle.
prcpg10{_}1473{_}For the Holy Land I am vowed to fight, and thither would I go had I none with me but Fowen, my groom!"
prcpg10{_}1474{_}And withal his eye lit on Richard, with a look of certainty of response; of security that here was one to partake his genuine ardour, and of refreshment in the midst of his disgust with the selfish uncle and sluggish cousin.
prcpg10{_}1475{_}That look, that half smile, made the youth's heart bound once more.
prcpg10{_}1476{_}Yes, with him he would go to the ends of the earth!
prcpg10{_}1477{_}What was the freedom of Guy's castle, to the following of such a lord and leader in such a cause?
prcpg10{_}1478{_}Richard could have thrown himself at his feet, and poured forth pledges of fidelity.
prcpg10{_}1479{_}But in ten minutes he was following home the unapproachable, silent, cold warrior.
prcpg10{_}1480{_}And the lack of any outlet for his aspirations turned them back upon themselves, with a strange sense of bitterness and almost of resentment.
prcpg10{_}1481{_}Leonillo alone, as the creature lay at his feet, and looked up into his face with eyes of deep wistful meaning, seemed to him to have any feeling for him; and Leonillo became the recipient of many an outpouring of something between discontent and melancholy.
prcpg10{_}1482{_}Leonillo, the sole remnant of his home!
prcpg10{_}1483{_}He burnt for that Holy Land where he was to win the name and fame lacking to him; but there was to be long delay.
prcpg10{_}1484{_}Fain would the Prince have proceeded at once to Palestine; but the Genoese, from whom, in the abeyance of the English navy, he had been obliged to hire his transports, absolutely refused to sail for the East until after the three winter months; and he was therefore obliged to remain in Sicily.
prcpg10{_}1485{_}King Charles invited him to spend Christmas at the court at Syracuse or Naples, in hopes, perhaps, of persuading him to the Greek expedition; but Edward was far too much displeased with the Angevin to accept his hospitality; recollecting, perhaps, that such a sojourn had been little beneficial to his great- uncle Coeur de Lion's army.
prcpg10{_}1486{_}He decided upon staying where he was, in the remotest corner of Sicily, and keeping his three hundred crusaders as much to themselves and to strict military discipline as possible, maintaining them at his own cost, and avoiding as far as he could all transactions with the cruel and violent Provencal adventurers, with whom Charles had filled the island.
prcpg10{_}1487{_}Thus Richard found his hopes of obtaining further intelligence about his brothers entirely passing away.
prcpg10{_}1488{_}He did, indeed, venture on one day saying to the Prince, "My Lord, I hear that my brother Guy hath become a Neapolitan count!"
prcpg10{_}1489{_}"A Tuscan robber would be nearer the mark!"
prcpg10{_}1490{_}coldly replied Edward.
prcpg10{_}1491{_}"And," added Richard, "methought, while the host is in winter quarters, I would venture on craving your license, my Lord, to visit him?"
prcpg10{_}1492{_}"Thou hast thy choice, Richard," answered the Prince, with grave displeasure; "loyalty and honour with me, or lawlessness and violence with thy brother.
prcpg10{_}1493{_}Both cannot be thine!"
prcpg10{_}1494{_}And returning to his study of the Lais of Marie de France, he made it evident that he would hear no more, and left Richard to a sharp struggle; in which hot irritation and wounded feeling would have carried him away at once from the stern superior who required the sacrifice of all his family, and gave not a word of sympathy in return.
prcpg10{_}1495{_}It was the crusading vow alone that detained the youth.
prcpg10{_}1496{_}He could not throw away his pledge to the wars of the Cross, and it was plain that if he went now to seek out Guy, he should never be allowed to return to the crusading army.
prcpg10{_}1497{_}But that vow once fulfilled, proud Edward should see, that not merely sufferance but friendliness was needed to bind the son of his father's sister to his service.
prcpg10{_}1498{_}The brother at Bednall Green was right, this bondage was worse than beggary.
prcpg10{_}1499{_}Nor, under the influence of these feelings, had Richard's service the alacrity and affection for which it had once been remarkable:  the Prince rebuked his short-comings unsparingly, and thus added to the sense of injury that had caused them; Hamlyn de Valence sneered, and Dame Idonea took good care to point out both the youth's neglects and his sullenness, and to whisper significantly that she did not wonder, considering the stock he came of.
prcpg10{_}1500{_}A soothing word or gentle excuse from the kind-hearted Princess were the only gleams of comfort that rendered the present state of things endurable.
prcpg10{_}1501{_}Just after Christmas arrived a vessel with reinforcements from home.
prcpg10{_}1502{_}Among them came a small body of Hospitaliers, with the novice Raynal at their head, now a full-blown knight, in dazzling scarlet and white, as Sir Reginald Ferrers.
prcpg10{_}1503{_}Richard at once recognized him, when he came to present himself to the Prince, and was very desirous of learning whether he knew aught of that other brother, so mysteriously hidden in obscurity.
prcpg10{_}1504{_}Sir Raynal on his side seemed to share the desire; he exchanged a friendly glance with the page, and when the formality of the reception was over sought him out, saying, "I have a greeting for you, Master Fowen."
prcpg10{_}1505{_}"From Sir Robert Darcy?"
prcpg10{_}1506{_}asked Richard.
prcpg10{_}1507{_}"How fares it with the kind old knight?"
prcpg10{_}1508{_}"Excellent well!
prcpg10{_}1509{_}Nay, nothing fares amiss with Father Robert!"
prcpg10{_}1510{_}said the young knight, smiling.
prcpg10{_}1511{_}"Everything is the very best that could have befallen him--to hear him speak.
prcpg10{_}1512{_}He is the very sunshine of the Spital, and had he been ordered on this Crusade, I think all the hamlets round would have risen to withhold him."
prcpg10{_}1513{_}"Ah!"
prcpg10{_}1514{_}said Richard, hoping he was acting indifference; "said he aught of the little maiden with the blind father?"
prcpg10{_}1515{_}"Pretty Bessee and Blind Hal of Bednall Green?
prcpg10{_}1516{_}Verily, that was the purport of my message.
prcpg10{_}1517{_}The poor knave hath been sorely sick and more cracked than ever this autumn; insomuch that Father Robert spent whole nights with him; and though he be better now, and as much in his senses as e'er he will be, such another access is like to make an end of him.
prcpg10{_}1518{_}Now, Father Robert saith that you, Sir Page, know who the poor man is by birth, and that he prays you to send him word what had best be done with the child, in case either of his death or of his getting so frenzied as to be unable to take care of her."
prcpg10{_}1519{_}"Send him word!"
prcpg10{_}1520{_}repeated Richard in perplexity.
prcpg10{_}1521{_}"We shall certainly have some one returning soon to the Spital," replied Sir Raynal.
prcpg10{_}1522{_}"Indeed, methinks some of the princes will be like to return, for the old King of the Romans is failing fast, and King Henry implored that the Prince of Almayne would come to hearten him."
prcpg10{_}1523{_}"Then must I write to Sir Robert?"
prcpg10{_}1524{_}said Richard; "mine is scarce a message for word of mouth."
prcpg10{_}1525{_}"So he said it was like to be," returned the knight, "and he took thought to send you a slip of parchment, knowing, he said, that such things are not wont to be found in a crusader's budget.
prcpg10{_}1526{_}Moreover, if ink be wanting, he bade me tell you that there's a fish in these seas, with many arms, and very like the foul fiend, that carries a bag of ink as good as any scrivener s.  "I have seen the monster," said Richard, who had often been down to the beach to see the unlading of the fishermen's boats, and to share little John of Dunster's unfailing marvel, that the Mediterranean should produce such outlandish creatures, so alien to his Bristol Channel experiences.
prcpg10{_}1527{_}And the very next time the boats came in, Richard made his way to the shore, on the beautiful, rocky, broken coast; and presently encountered a sepia, which fully justified Sir Robert's comparison, lying at the bottom of a boat.
prcpg10{_}1528{_}The fisherman intended it for his own dinner, when all his choicer fish should have gone to supply the Friday's meal of the English chivalry; and he was a good deal amazed when the young gentleman, making his Provencal as like Sicilian as he could, began to traffic with him for it, and at last made him understand that it was only its ink-bag that he wanted.
prcpg10{_}1529{_}The said ink, secured in a shell, was brought home by Richard, together with a couple of the largest sea-bird's quills that he could find--and which he shaped with his dagger, as best he might, in remembrance of Father Adam de Marisco's writing lessons.
prcpg10{_}1530{_}He meditated what should be the language of his letter, which was not likely to be secure from the eyes of the few who could read it; and finally decided that English was the tongue known to the fewest readers, who, if they knew letters at all, were sure to be acquainted with French and Latin.
prcpg10{_}1531{_}On a strip of parchment, then, about nine inches long and three wide, he proceeded to indite, in upright cramped letters, with many contractions, nearly in such terms as these -   REVEREND AND KNIGHTLY FATHER,  The good ghostly father and knight, Sir Raynald Ferrers, hath borne to me your tidings of my brother's sickness, and of all your goodness to him--whereof I pray that our blessed Lady and good St. John may reward you, for I can only pray for you.
prcpg10{_}1532{_}Touching his poor little daughter, in case of his death or frenzy, which the Saints of their mercy forefend, I would entreat you of your goodness to place her in some nunnery, but without making known her name and quality until my return; so Heaven bring me home safe.
prcpg10{_}1533{_}But an if I should be slain in this Eastern land, then were it most for the little one's good to present her to the gracious lady Princess, by whom she would be most lovingly and naturally cared for; and would be more safe than with such as might shun to own her rights of blood and heirship.
prcpg10{_}1534{_}Commend me to my brother, if so be that he cares to hear of me; and tell him that Guy hath wedded the lady of a castle in the land of Italy.
prcpg10{_}1535{_}And so praying you, ghostly father, for your blessing, I greet you well, and rest your grateful bedesman and servant,  RICHARD OF LEICESTER.
prcpg10{_}1536{_}Given at the Prince's camp at Drepanum, in the realm of Sicilia, on the octave of the Epiphany, in the year of grace MCCLXX.
prcpg10{_}1537{_}; and so our Lord have you heartily in His keeping.
prcpg10{_}1538{_}Letter-writing was a mighty task; and Richard's extemporary implements were not of the best.
prcpg10{_}1539{_}He laboured hard over his composition, kneeling against a chest in the tent.
prcpg10{_}1540{_}When at length he raised his head, he encountered a face full of the most utter amazement.
prcpg10{_}1541{_}Little John of Dunster had come into the tent, and stood gazing at him with open eyes and gaping mouth, as if he were perpetrating an incantation.
prcpg10{_}1542{_}Richard could not help laughing.
prcpg10{_}1543{_}"Why, Jack, dost think I am framing a spell for thee?"
prcpg10{_}1544{_}"Writing!"
prcpg10{_}1545{_}gasped John, relieving his distended mouth by at length closing it.
prcpg10{_}1546{_}"Wherefore not?
prcpg10{_}1547{_}Did not I see the chaplain teaching thee to write at Guildford?"
prcpg10{_}1548{_}"Ay--but that was when I was a babe!
prcpg10{_}1549{_}Writing!
prcpg10{_}1550{_}Why, my father never writes!"
prcpg10{_}1551{_}"But the Prince does.
prcpg10{_}1552{_}Thou hast seen him write.
prcpg10{_}1553{_}Come now," added Richard:  "if thou wilt, I will help thee to write a letter to send thy greetings home to Dunster.
prcpg10{_}1554{_}Thy father and mother will be right glad to hear thou hast 'scaped that African fever."
prcpg10{_}1555{_}"They!--They'd think me no better than a French monk!"
prcpg10{_}1556{_}said John.
prcpg10{_}1557{_}"And none of them could read it either!
prcpg10{_}1558{_}I'll never write!
prcpg10{_}1559{_}My grandsire only set his cross to the great charter!"
prcpg10{_}1560{_}And John retreated--in fear perhaps that Richard would sully his manhood with a writing lesson!
prcpg10{_}1561{_}The letter was rolled up in a scroll, bound with a silken thread, and committed to the charge of Sir Raynald Ferrers, who was going shortly to be commandery of his Order at Castel San Giovanni, whence he had no doubt of being able to send the letter safely to Sir Robert Darcy, at the Grand Priory.
prcpg10{_}1562{_}It would perhaps have been more expeditious to have intrusted the letter to one of the suite of Prince Henry of Almayne, who had been recalled by the tidings of the state of his father's health; but Richard dreaded betraying his brother's secret too much to venture on confiding the missive to any of this party--none of whom were indeed likely to wish to oblige him.
prcpg10{_}1563{_}Hamlyn de Valence was going with Henry as his esquire; and his absence seemed to Richard like the beginning of better days.
prcpg10{_}1564{_}CHAPTER IX--ASH WEDNESDAY    "Mostrocci un ombra da l' un canto sola Dicendo 'Colui feese in grembo a Dio Lo cuor che'n su Tamigi ancor si cola.'"
prcpg10{_}1565{_}DANTE.
prcpg10{_}1566{_}Inferno.
prcpg10{_}1567{_}Shrovetide had come, and the Prince had, before leaving Trapani, been taking some share in the entertainments of the Carnival.
prcpg10{_}1568{_}Personally, his grave reserve made gaieties distasteful to him; and the disastrous commencement of the Crusade weighed on his spirits.
prcpg10{_}1569{_}But when state and show were necessary, he provided for them with royal bounty and magnificence, and caused them to be regulated with the admirable taste of that age of exceeding beauty in which he lived.
prcpg10{_}1570{_}Thus, in this festal season, banquets were provided, and military shows took place, for the benefit of the Sicilian nobility and of the citizens of Trapani, on such a scale, that the English rose high in general esteem; and many were the secret wishes that Edmund of Lancaster rather than Charles of Anjou had been able to make good the grant from the Pope.
prcpg10{_}1571{_}Splendid were the displays, and no slight toil did they involve on the part of the immediate train of the Prince, few in number as they were, and destitute of the appliances of the resident court.
prcpg10{_}1572{_}Richard hurrying hither and thither, and waiting upon every one, had little of the diversion of the affair; but he would willingly have taken treble the care and toil in the relief it was to be free from the prying mistrustful eyes of Hamlyn de Valence.
prcpg10{_}1573{_}Looking after little John of Dunster was, however, no small part of his trouble; the urchin was so certain to get into some mischief if left to himself-- now treading on a lady's train, now upsetting a flagon of wine, now nearly impaling himself upon the point of a whole spitful of ortolans that were being handed round to the company, now becoming uncivilly deaf upon his French ear.
prcpg10{_}1574{_}Altogether, it was a relief to Richard's mind when he stumbled upon the little fellow fast asleep, even though it was in the middle of the Princess's violet velvet and ermine mantle, which she had laid down in order to tread a stately measure with Sire Guillaume de Porceles.
prcpg10{_}1575{_}After all Richard's exertions that evening, it was no wonder that the morning found him fast asleep at the unexampled hour of eight!
prcpg10{_}1576{_}His wakening was a strange one.
prcpg10{_}1577{_}His little fellow-page was standing beside him with a strange frightened yet important air.
prcpg10{_}1578{_}"What is the matter, John?
prcpg10{_}1579{_}It is late?
prcpg10{_}1580{_}Is the Prince gone to Mass?
prcpg10{_}1581{_}Has he missed me?"
prcpg10{_}1582{_}cried Richard, starting up in dismay, for unpunctuality was a great offence with Edward.
prcpg10{_}1583{_}"He is gone to Mass," said John, "but, before he comes back," he came near and lowered his voice, "Hob Longbow sent me to say you had better flee."
prcpg10{_}1584{_}"Flee!
prcpg10{_}1585{_}Boy, why should I flee?
prcpg10{_}1586{_}Are YOUR senses fleeing?"
prcpg10{_}1587{_}"O Richard," cried John, his face clearing up, "then it is not true!
prcpg10{_}1588{_}You are not one of the traitor Montforts!"
prcpg10{_}1589{_}"If I were a hundred Montforts, what has that to do with it?"
prcpg10{_}1590{_}"Then all is well," exclaimed the boy.
prcpg10{_}1591{_}"I said you were no such thing!
prcpg10{_}1592{_}I'll tell Hob he lied in his throat."
prcpg10{_}1593{_}"If he said I was a traitor, verily he did; but as to being a Montfort--But, how now, John, what means all this?"
prcpg10{_}1594{_}"Then it is so!
prcpg10{_}1595{_}O Richard, Richard, you cannot be one of them!
prcpg10{_}1596{_}You cannot have written that letter to warn them to murder Prince Henry."
prcpg10{_}1597{_}"To murder Prince Henry!"
prcpg10{_}1598{_}Richard stood transfixed.
prcpg10{_}1599{_}"Not the Prince's little son!"
prcpg10{_}1600{_}"Oh no, Prince Henry of Almayne!
prcpg10{_}1601{_}At Viterbo!
prcpg10{_}1602{_}Hamlyn de Valence saw it.
prcpg10{_}1603{_}He is come back.
prcpg10{_}1604{_}It was in the Cathedral.
prcpg10{_}1605{_}O Richard--at the elevation of the Host!
prcpg10{_}1606{_}Guy and Simon de Montfort fell on him, stabbed him to the heart, and rushed out.
prcpg10{_}1607{_}Then they came back again, and dragged him by the hair of his head into the mire, and shouted that so their father had been dragged through the streets of Evesham.
prcpg10{_}1608{_}And then they went off to the Maremma!
prcpg10{_}1609{_}And," continued the boy breathlessly, "Hob Long-bow is on guard, and he bade me tell you, that for love of your father he will let you pass; and then you can hide; if only you can go ere the Prince comes forth."
prcpg10{_}1610{_}"Hide!
prcpg10{_}1611{_}Wherefore should I hide?
prcpg10{_}1612{_}This is most horrible, but it is no deed of mine!"
prcpg10{_}1613{_}said Richard.
prcpg10{_}1614{_}"Who dares to think it is?"
prcpg10{_}1615{_}"Then you are none of them!
prcpg10{_}1616{_}You had no part in it!
prcpg10{_}1617{_}I shall tell Hob he is a villain--"  "Stay," said Richard, laying a detaining hand on the boy.
prcpg10{_}1618{_}"Why does Hob think me in danger?
prcpg10{_}1619{_}Is anything stirring against me?"
prcpg10{_}1620{_}"They all--all of poor Prince Henry's meine, that are come back with Hamlyn--say that you are a Montfort too, and--oh!
prcpg10{_}1621{_}do not look so fierce!--that you sent a letter to warn your brethren where to meet, and fall on the Prince.
prcpg10{_}1622{_}And the murderers being fled, they are keen to have your life; and, Richard, you know I saw you write the letter."
prcpg10{_}1623{_}"That you saw me write a letter, is as certain as that my name is Montfort," said Richard, "but I am not therefore leagued with traitors or murderers!
prcpg10{_}1624{_}In the church, saidst thou?
prcpg10{_}1625{_}Oh, well that the Prince forbade me to visit Guy!"
prcpg10{_}1626{_}"Then you will not flee?"
prcpg10{_}1627{_}"No, forsooth.
prcpg10{_}1628{_}I will stay and prove my innocence."
prcpg10{_}1629{_}"But you are a Montfort!
prcpg10{_}1630{_}And I saw you write the letter."
prcpg10{_}1631{_}"Did you speak of my having written the letter?"
prcpg10{_}1632{_}asked Richard, pausing.
prcpg10{_}1633{_}The boy hung his head, and muttered something about Dame Idonea.
prcpg10{_}1634{_}By this time, even if Richard had thought of flight, it would have been impossible.
prcpg10{_}1635{_}Two archers made their presence apparent at the entrance of the tent, and in brief gruff tones informed Richard that the Prince required his presence.
prcpg10{_}1636{_}The space between his tent and the royal pavilion was short, but in those few steps Richard had time to glance over the dangers of his position, and take up his resolution though with a certain stunned sense that nothing could be before the member of a proscribed family, but failure, suspicion, and ruin.
prcpg10{_}1637{_}The two brothers, Edward and Edmund, with the Earl of Gloucester, and their other chief councillors, were assembled; and there were looks of deep concern on the faces of all, making Edward's more than ever like a rigid marble statue; while Edmund had evidently been weeping bitterly, though his features were full of fierce indignation.
prcpg10{_}1638{_}Hamlyn de Valence, and a few other members of the murdered Prince's suite, stood near in deep mourning suits.
prcpg10{_}1639{_}"Richard de Montfort," said Prince Edward, looking at him with a sorrowful reproachful sternness that went to his heart, "we have sent for you to answer for yourself, on a grave charge.
prcpg10{_}1640{_}You have heard of that which has befallen?"
prcpg10{_}1641{_}"I have heard, my Lord, of a foul crime which my soul abhors.
prcpg10{_}1642{_}I trust none present here think me capable of sharing in it!
prcpg10{_}1643{_}Whoever dares to accuse me, shall be answered by my sword!"
prcpg10{_}1644{_}and he glanced fiercely at Hamlyn.
prcpg10{_}1645{_}"Hold!"
prcpg10{_}1646{_}said Edward severely, "no one is so senseless as to accuse you of taking actual part in a crime that took place beyond the sea; but there is only too much reason to believe that you have been tampered with by your brothers."
prcpg10{_}1647{_}Then, as his brother Edmund made some suggestion to him, he added, "Is John de Mohun of Dunster here?"
prcpg10{_}1648{_}"Yea, my Lord," said the little boy, coming forward, with a flush on his face, and a bold though wistful look, "but verily Richard is no traitor, be he who he may!"
prcpg10{_}1649{_}"That is not what we wished to ask of you," said the Prince, too sad and earnest to be amused even for a moment.
prcpg10{_}1650{_}"Tell us whom you said, even now, you had seen in the tent you shared with him in Africa."
prcpg10{_}1651{_}"I said I had seen his wraith," said John.
prcpg10{_}1652{_}No smile lighted upon the Prince's features; they were as serious as those of the boy, as he commented, "His likeness--his exact likeness- -you mean."
prcpg10{_}1653{_}"Ay," said the boy; "but Richard proved to me after, that it had been less tall, and was bearded likewise.
prcpg10{_}1654{_}So I hoped it did not bode him ill."  "Worse, I fear, than if it had in sooth been his double," said Gloucester to Prince Edmund.
prcpg10{_}1655{_}The Prince added the question whether this visitor had spoken; and John related the inquiry for Richard by the name of Montfort, and his own reply, which elicited a murmur of amused applause among the bystanders.
prcpg10{_}1656{_}The Prince, however, continued in the same grave manner to draw from the little witness his account of Richard's injunction to secresy; and then asked about the letter-writing, of which John gave his plain account.
prcpg10{_}1657{_}The Prince then said, "Speak now, Hamlyn."
prcpg10{_}1658{_}"This, then, I have to add, my Lord, that I, as all the world, remarked that Richard de Montfort consorted much with Sir Reginald de Ferrieres, who, as we all remember, is the son of a family deeply concerned in the Mad Parliament.
prcpg10{_}1659{_}By Sir Reginald, on his arrival at Castel San Giovanni, a messenger is despatched, bearing letters to the Hospital at Florence, and it is immediately after his arrival there, that the two Montforts speed from the Maremma to the unhappy and bloody Mass at Viterbo."
prcpg10{_}1660{_}You hear, Richard!"
prcpg10{_}1661{_}said the Prince.
prcpg10{_}1662{_}"I bade you choose between me and your brothers.
prcpg10{_}1663{_}Had you believed me that you could not serve both, it had been better for you.
prcpg10{_}1664{_}I credit not that you incited them to the assassination; but your tidings led them to perpetrate it.
prcpg10{_}1665{_}I cannot retain the spy of the Montforts in my camp."
prcpg10{_}1666{_}"My Lord," said Richard, at last finding space for speech, "I deny all collusion with my brothers.
prcpg10{_}1667{_}I have neither seen, spoken with, nor sent to them by letter nor word."
prcpg10{_}1668{_}"Then to whom was this letter?"
prcpg10{_}1669{_}demanded the Prince.
prcpg10{_}1670{_}"To Sir Robert Darcy, the Grand Prior of England," answered Richard.
prcpg10{_}1671{_}A murmur of incredulous amazement was heard.
prcpg10{_}1672{_}"The purport?"
prcpg10{_}1673{_}continued Edward.
prcpg10{_}1674{_}"That, my Lord, it consorts not with my duty to tell."
prcpg10{_}1675{_}"Look here, Richard," interposed Gilbert of Gloucester, "this is an unlikely tale.
prcpg10{_}1676{_}You can have no cause for secresy, save in connection with these brothers; and if you will point to some way of clearing yourself of being art and part in this foul act of murder, you may be sent scot free from the camp; but if you wilfully maintain this denial, what can we do but treat you as a traitor?
prcpg10{_}1677{_}No obstinacy!
prcpg10{_}1678{_}What can a lad like you have to say to good old Sir Robert Darcy, that all the world might not know?"
prcpg10{_}1679{_}"My Lord of Gloucester," said Richard, "I am bound in honour not to reveal the matters between me and Sir Robert; I can only declare on the faith of a Christian gentleman that I have neither had, nor attempted to have, any dealings with either of my brothers, Guy or Simon; and if any man says I have, I will prove his falsehood on his body."
prcpg10{_}1680{_}And Richard flung down his glove before the Prince.
prcpg10{_}1681{_}At the same moment Hamlyn de Valence sprang forward.
prcpg10{_}1682{_}"Then, Richard de Montfort, I take up the gage.
prcpg10{_}1683{_}I give thee the lie in thy throat, and will prove on thy body that thou art a man-sworn traitor, in league with thy false brethren."
prcpg10{_}1684{_}"I commit me to the judgment of God," said Richard, looking upwards.
prcpg10{_}1685{_}"My Lord," said Hamlyn, "have we your permission to fight out the matter?"
prcpg10{_}1686{_}"You have," said Edward, "since to that holy judgment Richard hath appealed."
prcpg10{_}1687{_}But the Prince looked far from contented with the appeal.
prcpg10{_}1688{_}He allowed the preliminaries of place and time to be fixed without his interposition; and when the council broke up, he fixed his clear deep eyes upon Richard in a manner which seemed to the boy to upbraid him with the want of confidence, for which, however, he would not condescend to ask.
prcpg10{_}1689{_}Richard felt that, let the issue of the combat be what it would, he had lost that full trust on the part of the Prince, which had hitherto been his one drop of comfort; and if he were dismissed from the camp, he should be more than ever desolate, for his soul could scarce yet bring itself to grasp the horror of the crime of his brothers.
prcpg10{_}1690{_}The combat could not take place for two days--waiting, on one, in order that Hamlyn might have time to rest, and recover his full strength after his voyage, and the next, because it was Ash Wednesday.
prcpg10{_}1691{_}In the meantime Richard was left solitary; under no restraint, but universally avoided.
prcpg10{_}1692{_}The judicial combat did not make him uneasy; the two youths had often measured their strength together, and though Hamlyn was the elder, Richard was the taller, and had inherited something of the Plantagenet frame, so remarkable in those two   Lords of the biting axe and beamy spear,   "wide conquering Edward" and "Lion Richard"; and each believed in the righteousness of his own cause sufficiently to have implicit confidence that the right would be shown on his side.
prcpg10{_}1693{_}In fact, Richard soon understood that though Prince Edward, with a sense of the value of definite evidence far in advance of the time, and befitting the English Justinian, had only allowed the charge to be brought against him which could in a manner be substantiated, yet that the general belief went much further.
prcpg10{_}1694{_}Proved to be a Montfort, and to have written a letter, he was therefore convicted, by universal consent, of a league with his brothers for the revenge of their house; to have instigated the assassination at Viterbo, and to be only biding his time for the like act at Trapani.
prcpg10{_}1695{_}Even the Prince was deeply offended by his silence, and imputed it to no good motive; trust and affection were gone, and Richard felt no tie to retain him where he was, save his duty as a crusader.
prcpg10{_}1696{_}Let him fail in the combat, and the best he could look for would be to be ignominiously branded and expelled:  let him gain, and he much doubted whether, though the ordeal of battle was always respected, he would regain his former position.
prcpg10{_}1697{_}With keen suffering and indignation, he rebelled against Edward's harshness and distrust.
prcpg10{_}1698{_}He--who had brought him there--who ought to have known him better!
prcpg10{_}1699{_}Moreover, there was the crushing sense of the guilt of his brothers; guilt most horrible in its sacrilegious audacity, and doubly shocking to the feelings of a family where the grim sanctity of the first Simon de Montfort, and the enlightened devotion of the second, formed such a contrast to the savage outrage of him who now bore their name.
prcpg10{_}1700{_}Richard, as with bare feet and ashes whitening his dark locks he knelt on the cold stones of the dark Norman church at Trapani, wept hot and bitter tears of humiliation over the family crimes that had brought them so low; prayed in an agony for repentance for his brothers; and for himself, some opening for expiating their sin against at least the generous royal family.
prcpg10{_}1701{_}"O!
prcpg10{_}1702{_}could I but die for my Prince, and know that he forgave and they repented!"
prcpg10{_}1703{_}Only when on his way back to the camp was he sensible of the murmurs of censure at his hypocrisy in joining the penitential procession at all.
prcpg10{_}1704{_}Dame Idonea, in a complete suit of sackcloth, was informing her friends that she had made a vow not to wash her face till the whole adder brood of Montfort had been crushed; and that she trusted to see the beginning of justice done to-morrow.
prcpg10{_}1705{_}She had offered a candle to St. James to that effect, hoping to induce him to turn away his patronage from the family.
prcpg10{_}1706{_}Every one, knight or squire, shrank away from Richard, if he did but look towards them; and he was seriously discomfited by the difficulty of obtaining a godfather for the combat.
prcpg10{_}1707{_}No one chose even to be asked, lest they might be suspected of approving of the murder of Prince Henry; and the unhappy page re-entered his tent with the most desolate sense of being abandoned by heaven and man.
prcpg10{_}1708{_}Fastened upon the pole of the tent by an arrowhead, a small scroll of parchment met his eyes.
prcpg10{_}1709{_}He read in English--"A steed and a lance are ready for the lioncel who would rather avenge his father than lick the tyrant's feet.
prcpg10{_}1710{_}A guide awaits thee."
prcpg10{_}1711{_}Some weeks since, this might have been a tempting summons; but now the sickening sense of the sacrilegious murder, and of the life of outlawry utterly unrestrained, passed over Richard.
prcpg10{_}1712{_}Yet, if he should not accept the offer, what was before him?
prcpg10{_}1713{_}A shameful death, perhaps; if he failed in the ordeal, disgrace, captivity, or expulsion; if he succeeded, bondage and distrust for ever.
prcpg10{_}1714{_}Some new accusation!
prcpg10{_}1715{_}some deeper fall!
prcpg10{_}1716{_}There was a low growl from Leonillo; the hangings of the tent were raised, and an archer bending his head said, "A word with you, Sir."
prcpg10{_}1717{_}"Who art thou?"
prcpg10{_}1718{_}demanded Richard.
prcpg10{_}1719{_}"Hob Longbow, Sir.
prcpg10{_}1720{_}Remember you not old passages--in the forest, there--and Master Adam?"
prcpg10{_}1721{_}Richard did remember the archer in the days of his outlaw life, in a very different capacity.
prcpg10{_}1722{_}"You were grown so tall, Sir, and so hand and glove with the Longshanks, that Nick Dustifoot and I knew not an if it were yourself--but now your name is out, and the wind is in another quarter"--he grinned, then seeing Richard impatient of the approach to familiarity, "You did not know Nick Dustifoot?
prcpg10{_}1723{_}He was one of young Sir Simon's men-at-arms, you see, and took to the woods, like other folk, after Kenilworth was given up, till stout men were awanting for this Crusade.
prcpg10{_}1724{_}And he knew Sir Guy when he came to the camp yon by Tunis, and spake with him; moreover, he went in the train of him of Almayne to Viterbo, and had speech again with Sir Simon, who gave him this scroll.
prcpg10{_}1725{_}And if you will meet him at the Syren's Rock to-night, my Lord Richard, he will bring you to those who will conduct you to Sir Guy's brave castle, where he laughs kings and counts to scorn!
prcpg10{_}1726{_}We have the guard, and will see you safe past the gates of the camp."
prcpg10{_}1727{_}The way to liberty was open:  Richard deliberated.
prcpg10{_}1728{_}The atmosphere of distrust and suspicion under the Prince's coldness was well-nigh unbearable.
prcpg10{_}1729{_}Danger faced him for the next day!
prcpg10{_}1730{_}Disgrace was everywhere.
prcpg10{_}1731{_}Should he leave it behind, where, at least, he would not hear and feel it?
prcpg10{_}1732{_}Should he, when all had turned from him, meet a brotherly welcome?
prcpg10{_}1733{_}Then came back on him the thought of what Simon and Guy had made themselves; the thought of his father's grief at former doings of theirs, which had fallen so far short of the atrocity of this.
prcpg10{_}1734{_}He knew that his father had rather have seen each one of his five sons slain, or helpless cripples like the firstborn, than have been thus avenged.
prcpg10{_}1735{_}Nay, had he this morning prayed for the pardon of a crime, to which he would thus become a consenting party?
prcpg10{_}1736{_}He looked up resolutely.
prcpg10{_}1737{_}"No, Hob Longbow.
prcpg10{_}1738{_}Hap what hap, my part can never be with those who have stained the Church with blood.
prcpg10{_}1739{_}Let my brothers know that my heart yearned to them before, but now all is over between us.
prcpg10{_}1740{_}I can only bear the doom they have brought upon me!"
prcpg10{_}1741{_}It was not possible to remain and argue.
prcpg10{_}1742{_}A tent was a dangerous place for secret conferences, and Hob Longbow could only growl, "As you will, Sir.
prcpg10{_}1743{_}Now nor you nor any one else can say I have not done my charge."
prcpg10{_}1744{_}"Alack, alack!"
prcpg10{_}1745{_}sighed Richard, "would that, my honour once redeemed, Hamlyn might make an end of me!
prcpg10{_}1746{_}But for thee, my poor Leonillo, I have no comforter or friend!"
prcpg10{_}1747{_}and he flung his arms round the dog's neck.
prcpg10{_}1748{_}CHAPTER X--THE COMBAT    "And now with sae sharp of steele They 'gan to lay on load."
prcpg10{_}1749{_}Sir Cauline.
prcpg10{_}1750{_}Heavy-hearted and pale-cheeked with his rigidly observed fast, Richard armed himself in early morning, and set forth to the chapel tent, where the previous solemnities had to be observed.
prcpg10{_}1751{_}He had made up his mind to make an earnest appeal to the Earl of Gloucester, for the sake of the old friendship with his father, to become his godfather in the combat, as one whose character stood too high to be injured by connection with him.
prcpg10{_}1752{_}Even this plan was frustrated, for Hamlyn de Valence entered, led by Earl Gilbert as his sponsor.
prcpg10{_}1753{_}Should he turn to his one other friend, the Prince himself?
prcpg10{_}1754{_}Nay, the Prince was umpire and judge.
prcpg10{_}1755{_}Never stood warrior so lonely.
prcpg10{_}1756{_}Little John of Dunster crept up to his side; and but for fear of injuring the child, he would almost have asked him to be his sponsor.
prcpg10{_}1757{_}At that moment, however, the tramp of horses' feet was heard, and Sir Reginald de Ferrieres, with his squires, galloped up to the tent.
prcpg10{_}1758{_}The young Hospitalier held out his hand cordially.
prcpg10{_}1759{_}"In time, I hope," said he; "I have ridden ever since Lauds at Castel San Giovanni, hoping to be with you, so as to stand by you in this matter."
prcpg10{_}1760{_}"It was kindly done of you," said Richard, tears of gratitude swelling in his eyes, as he wrung Sir Raynald's hand.
prcpg10{_}1761{_}"I have not even a godfather for the fight!
prcpg10{_}1762{_}How could you know of my need?"
prcpg10{_}1763{_}"Some of our brethren came over from the camp, for our Ash Wednesday procession, and spoke of the stress you were in--that your Montfort lineage was out, and that you were thought to have writ a letter--but stay, there's no time for words; methinks here's the Prince and all his train."
prcpg10{_}1764{_}Sir Raynald went through the solemnity of presenting Richard de Montfort as about to fight in defence of his own innocence.
prcpg10{_}1765{_}The Prince coldly accepted the presentation.
prcpg10{_}1766{_}Richard knew that Sir Raynald was deemed anything but a satisfactory sponsor; but the young knight's hearty sympathy, a sort of radiance caught from good old Sir Robert, was too comforting not to be reposed on.
prcpg10{_}1767{_}Each champion then confessed.
prcpg10{_}1768{_}Raynald heard Richard's shrift, and nearly wept over it--it was the first the young priestly knight had received, and he could scarcely clear his voice to speak the words of absolution.
prcpg10{_}1769{_}Even as they left the confessional, he grasped Richard's hand and said, "Cast in thy lot with us!
prcpg10{_}1770{_}St. John will find thee father and home and brethren!"
prcpg10{_}1771{_}And a gleam of joy and hope flashed on the youth's heart, and shone brighter as he participated in the solemn Mass in preparation for the combat.
prcpg10{_}1772{_}This over, each champion made oath of the justice of his quarrel in the hands of his godfather before the Prince:  Hamlyn de Valence swearing that to the best of his belief, Richard de Montfort was a traitor, in league with his brothers, and art and part in the murder of Prince Henry of Almayne, and offering to prove it on his body; while on the other hand Richard swore that he was a true and faithful liegeman to the King, free from all intercourse with his brethren, and sackless of the death of Prince Henry.
prcpg10{_}1773{_}Then each mounted on horseback, the trumpets sounded, the sponsors led them to their places, and the Prince's clear voice exclaimed, "And so God show the right."
prcpg10{_}1774{_}One glance of pitying sympathy would have filled Richard's arm with fresh vigour.
prcpg10{_}1775{_}The two youths closed with shivered lances, and horses reeling from the shock.
prcpg10{_}1776{_}Backing their steeds, each received a fresh lance.
prcpg10{_}1777{_}Again they met; Richard felt the point of Hamlyn's lance glint against his breastplate, glide down, enter, make its way into his flesh; but at the same instant his lance was pushing, driving, bearing on Hamlyn before him; the sheer force in his Plantagenet shoulders was telling now, the very pain seemed as it were to add to the energy with which he pressed on--on, till the hostile spear dropped from his own side, and Hamlyn was borne backwards over the croup of the staggering horse, till he fell with crashing ringing armour upon the ground.
prcpg10{_}1778{_}Little John clapped his hands, and shouted for joy; but no one responded.
prcpg10{_}1779{_}Richard leapt down in another second, and stood over him.
prcpg10{_}1780{_}"Yield thee, Hamlyn de Valence.
prcpg10{_}1781{_}Confess that thou hast slandered me with an ungrounded accusation."
prcpg10{_}1782{_}Hamlyn had no choice.
prcpg10{_}1783{_}"Let me rise," he said sullenly; "I will confess, so thou letst me open my visor."
prcpg10{_}1784{_}And Richard standing aside, Hamlyn spoke out in a dogged formal tone.
prcpg10{_}1785{_}"I hereby own, that by the judgment of Heaven, Richard de Montfort hath cleared himself of all share in the foul murder of Lord Henry, whose soul Heaven assoilzie.
prcpg10{_}1786{_}Also that he hath disproven the charge of leaguing with his brethren."
prcpg10{_}1787{_}Richard was the victor, but where were the gratulations?
prcpg10{_}1788{_}Young John's hearty but slender hurrah was lost in the general silence.
prcpg10{_}1789{_}The Prince reared his stately form, and said, "The judgment of Heaven is final.
prcpg10{_}1790{_}Richard de Montfort is pronounced free of all penalty for treason in the matter of the death of our dear cousin, and is free to go where he will."
prcpg10{_}1791{_}Cold as ice was the Prince's face.
prcpg10{_}1792{_}That Richard meant murder to Henry, he had never believed; but that he had hankered after his brothers, and held dangerous communings with them, was evidently still credited and unforgiven.
prcpg10{_}1793{_}The very form of words was a dismissal--and the youth's heart was wrung.
prcpg10{_}1794{_}He stood, looking earnestly up as the Prince moved from his place, without a glance towards him.
prcpg10{_}1795{_}The next moment Raynald's kind hand was on his shoulder, and his voice saying, "Well fought, brother, a brave stroke!
prcpg10{_}1796{_}Come with me, thou art hurt."
prcpg10{_}1797{_}"Would it were to the death!"
prcpg10{_}1798{_}murmured Richard dreamily, as Raynald, throwing his arm round him, led him away; but before they had reached the tent there was a plunging rush and scampering behind them, and John of Dunster came dashing up.
prcpg10{_}1799{_}"I knew it!
prcpg10{_}1800{_}I knew it!"
prcpg10{_}1801{_}he cried.
prcpg10{_}1802{_}"I knew he would overset spiteful Hamlyn!
prcpg10{_}1803{_}Hurrah!
prcpg10{_}1804{_}They can't keep me away now, Richard--now the judgment of Heaven has gone for you!"
prcpg10{_}1805{_}Richard smiled, and put his gauntleted hand caressingly on the boy's shoulder.
prcpg10{_}1806{_}"I was afraid," added John, "that you would think me like the rest of them.
prcpg10{_}1807{_}Miscreants, all!
prcpg10{_}1808{_}Not one would shout for you--you, the victor!
prcpg10{_}1809{_}They don't heed the judgment of Heaven one jot.
prcpg10{_}1810{_}And that's what they call being warriors of the Cross!
prcpg10{_}1811{_}If the Prince were a true-born Englishman, he would be ashamed of himself.
prcpg10{_}1812{_}But never heed, Richard.
prcpg10{_}1813{_}Why don't you speak to me?
prcpg10{_}1814{_}Are you angered that I told of the letter?
prcpg10{_}1815{_}Indeed, I never guessed--"  "Hush, varlet," said Sir Raynald, "see you not that he has neither breath nor voice to speak?
prcpg10{_}1816{_}If you wish to do him a service, hie to our tents--down yonder, to the east, where you see the eight-pointed cross--"  "I know, Sir," said John, perfectly civil on hearing accents as English as his own.
prcpg10{_}1817{_}"And bring up Brother Bartlemy, he is a better infirmarer than I.
prcpg10{_}1818{_}Bid him from me bring his salves and bandages."
prcpg10{_}1819{_}Richard was barely conscious when he reached the tent, as much from rigid fasting and sleeplessness as from the actual loss of blood.
prcpg10{_}1820{_}His friend disarmed him tenderly, and revived him with bread and wine, silencing a half-murmured scruple about Lenten diet with the dispensation due to sickness.
prcpg10{_}1821{_}The wound was not likely to be serious or disabling, and the cares of the Hospitalier and his infirmarer had presently set their patient so much at ease that he dropped into a sound sleep, having scarcely said a word, beyond a few faintly uttered thanks, since he had fought the combat.
prcpg10{_}1822{_}At first his sleep was profound, but by and by the associations of blows and wounds carried him back to the field of Evesham.
prcpg10{_}1823{_}The wild melee was renewed, he heard the voice of his father, but always in that strange distressing manner peculiar to dreams of the departed, always far away, and just beyond his reach, ever just about to give him the succour he needed, but ever withheld.
prcpg10{_}1824{_}The thunderstorm that broke over the contending armies roared again in his ears; and then again recurred the calm still night, when he had lain helpless on the battle-field; even the caress of Leonillo, and his low growl, were vividly repeated; but as the dog moved, it was to Richard as if the form of his father rose up in its armour from the dark field, and said in a deep hollow voice, "Well fought, my son; I will give thee knighthood."
prcpg10{_}1825{_}Then Richard thought he was kneeling before his father, and hearing that same voice saying, "My son, be true and loyal.
prcpg10{_}1826{_}In the name of God and St. James.
prcpg10{_}1827{_}I dub thee knight of death!"
prcpg10{_}1828{_}and looking up, he beheld under the helmet, not Simon de Montfort's face but the Prince's.
prcpg10{_}1829{_}He awoke with a start of disappointment--and there stood Edward himself, leaning against the tent-pole, looking down at him!
prcpg10{_}1830{_}He sprang on his feet, scarcely knowing whether he slept or woke; but Edward said, in that voice that at times was so ineffably sweet, "Be still, Richard; I fear me thou hast suffered a wrong, and I am come to repair it, as far as I can!
prcpg10{_}1831{_}Lay thee down again."
prcpg10{_}1832{_}And the Prince seated himself on the oaken chest; while Richard, after a few words, sat down on his couch.
prcpg10{_}1833{_}"Is this the letter about which there has been such a coil?"
prcpg10{_}1834{_}said Edward, giving him the scroll in its sepia ink.
prcpg10{_}1835{_}"It is!"
prcpg10{_}1836{_}replied Richard in amazement and dismay.
prcpg10{_}1837{_}"The only letter thou didst write?"
prcpg10{_}1838{_}"The only one," repeated Richard.
prcpg10{_}1839{_}"And," added Edward, "it concerns thy brother Henry.
prcpg10{_}1840{_}Richard turned even paler than before, and could not suppress a gasp of dismay.
prcpg10{_}1841{_}"My Lord, make me not forsworn!"
prcpg10{_}1842{_}"Listen to me, Richard," said Edward.
prcpg10{_}1843{_}"My sweet lady gave me no rest about thee.
prcpg10{_}1844{_}She held that I had withdrawn my trust over lightly, for what was no blame to thine heart; and that having set thee here apart from thy natural friends, we owed thee more notice than I have been wont to think wholesome for untried striplings.
prcpg10{_}1845{_}Others, and I among them, held that Raynald Ferrers' friendship and countenance showed thee stubbornly set on old connections, and many thought the letter to the Grand Prior Darcy a mere excuse.
prcpg10{_}1846{_}But when Hamlyn fell, and I still held that thou wert merely cleared from wilful share in the deadly crime of which I had never held thee guilty, then she spake more earnestly.
prcpg10{_}1847{_}She of her own will sent for Raynald Ferrers to our tent, and called me to speak with him, sure that, even though his family had been our foes, he was too honourable a knight to have espoused thy cause without good reason.
prcpg10{_}1848{_}Then it was that he told us of thine interest for the blind beggar whose child thou didst save, and of the Grand Prior's message.
prcpg10{_}1849{_}Also, as full exculpation of thee, he gave me the letter, which, having failed to find a home-bound messenger at San Giovanni, he had brought back to the camp.
prcpg10{_}1850{_}And now, Richard, what can I say more, than that I did thee wrong, and pray thee to give me thy hand in pardon?"
prcpg10{_}1851{_}Richard hid his face and sobbed, completely overwhelmed by the simple dignity of the humility of such a man as Edward.
prcpg10{_}1852{_}He held the Prince's hand to his lips, and exclaimed, "Oh, how--how could I have ever felt discontent, or faltered?
prcpg10{_}1853{_}not in truth--oh, no--but in trust and patience?
prcpg10{_}1854{_}Oh!
prcpg10{_}1855{_}my Lord, that I could die for you!"
prcpg10{_}1856{_}"Not yet," said Edward, smiling; "we have much to do together first.
prcpg10{_}1857{_}And now tell me, Richard, this beggar is indeed Henry?"
prcpg10{_}1858{_}Richard hung his head.
prcpg10{_}1859{_}"What, thou mayst not betray him?"
prcpg10{_}1860{_}"I am under an oath, my Lord."
prcpg10{_}1861{_}"Nay, I know well-nigh all, Richard.
prcpg10{_}1862{_}I did indeed see my dear old comrade laid in Evesham Church, so as it broke my heart to see him, bleeding from many wounds, and even his hand lopped by the savage Mortimers.
prcpg10{_}1863{_}Then, as I bent down, and gave his brow a last kiss, it struck me, for a moment, that the touch was not that of a dead man's skin.
prcpg10{_}1864{_}But I looked again at the deadly wounds of head and breast, and thought it would be but cruelty to strive to bring back the glimmer of life only to--to see the ruin of his house; and all that he could not be saved from.
prcpg10{_}1865{_}O Richard, to no man in either host could the day of Evesham have been so sore, as to me, who had to sit in the gate, to gladden men's hearts, like holy King David, when he would fain have been weeping for his son!
prcpg10{_}1866{_}But in early morning came Abbot William of Whitchurch to my chamber, and with much secrecy told me that the corpse of Henry de Montfort had been stolen from the church by night, praying me to excuse that the monks, wearied out with the day of alarms, and the care of our wounded, had not kept better watch.
prcpg10{_}1867{_}Then knew I that some one had been less faithless than I, and I hoped that poor Henry was at least dying in peace; I had never deemed that he could survive.
prcpg10{_}1868{_}But when I saw thy billet, and heard Ferrers' tale, I had no further doubt, remembering likewise how strangely familiar was the face of that little one at Westminster."
prcpg10{_}1869{_}"Yes, my Lord, it was even as a strange, wild, wilful, blind beggar that I found poor Henry; and heavy was the curse he laid me under, should I make him known to you.
prcpg10{_}1870{_}He calls himself thus a freer and happier man than he could be even were he pardoned and reinstated; and he can indulge his vein of mockery."
prcpg10{_}1871{_}"I dare be sworn that consoles him for all," said Edward, nearly laughing.
prcpg10{_}1872{_}"So long as he could utter his gibe, Henry little recked which way the world passed round him; and I trow he has found some mate of low degree, that he would be loth to produce in open day."
prcpg10{_}1873{_}"Not so, my Lord:  it is so wild a tale of true love that I can sometimes scarce believe a minstrel did not sing it to me!"
prcpg10{_}1874{_}And Richard told the history of Isabel Mortimer's fidelity.
prcpg10{_}1875{_}The Prince was deeply touched, and then remembered the marked manner in which the Baron of Mortimer had replied to his inquiry, in what convent he had bestowed Henry de Montfort's betrothed.
prcpg10{_}1876{_}"She is dead, my Lord, dead to us."
prcpg10{_}1877{_}Then he added suddenly, "So that black-eyed babe is the heiress of Leicester and all the honours of Montfort!"
